<div id="review_1240289445" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1240289445" />
    <a title="Phyllis Eisenstadt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14351728-phyllis-eisenstadt"><img alt="Phyllis Eisenstadt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535389778p2/14351728.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1240289445?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Phyllis Eisenstadt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Phyllis Eisenstadt" href="/user/show/14351728-phyllis-eisenstadt">Phyllis Eisenstadt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/8866365-an-american-tragedy">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1240289445" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13931740583633197026">"An American Tragedy," is a tale of conflicts, conscience, and polarized socioeconomic classes, from stark poverty to ostentatious luxury and opulence.  It is narrated by an omniscient author, which makes it as believable as if the readers were eye-witnesses.  It is also the personal story of Clyde, the protagonist -- a selfish, self-absorbed social climber who places more value upon wealth and luxury than upon human feelings.<br/><br/>The characters in this novel are so clearly portrayed that the reader </span>
  <span id="freeText13931740583633197026" style="display:none">"An American Tragedy," is a tale of conflicts, conscience, and polarized socioeconomic classes, from stark poverty to ostentatious luxury and opulence.  It is narrated by an omniscient author, which makes it as believable as if the readers were eye-witnesses.  It is also the personal story of Clyde, the protagonist -- a selfish, self-absorbed social climber who places more value upon wealth and luxury than upon human feelings.<br /><br />The characters in this novel are so clearly portrayed that the reader feels s/he would recognize them if seen in person.  Each character's words and actions are consistent with the picture Dreiser had previously painted of them.  Nothing is out of place in their demeanor.<br /><br />The courtroom scenes were dynamically descriptive, with the prosecuting attorney's brilliant piecing together of the nefarious incident.  One could easily say the construction and presentation of his case were not only a tribute to the justice system of that time, but also to human intelligence.  Marvelous!<br /><br />The author, Theodore Dreiser, is a master of writing and story-telling -- so much so that this was my second reading of the book.  He observes many details, from the smallest and (seemingly) most insignificant to the most important and major events -- yet another reason the descriptions of background, places, and mores come to life for the reader.<br /><br />At times, it may seem that the tale goes on and on without end, but be assured there is nothing superfluous about the details.  They all serve to place you right there in the middle of things, seeing what the narrator sees..<br /><br />Whether the outcome of this novel is to your liking or not, it is still worthy of profound appreciation.<br /><br />So pleased I decided to read it again!<br /><br /><br />Phyllis Eisenstadt</span>
  <a data-text-id="13931740583633197026" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/30278787?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Book2Dragon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Book2Dragon" href="/user/show/1431094-book2dragon">Book2Dragon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer30278787" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer696843359251955890">This may be the book that made me (gasp) a socialist and a skeptic of capitalism which is unregulated and unbridled. Dreiser is a wonderful writer. This book is highly recommended for today's America, especially in an election year.</span>

<div id="review_3910068" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3910068" />
    <a title="Kelly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/195890-kelly"><img alt="Kelly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1184617127p2/195890.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3910068?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kelly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kelly" href="/user/show/195890-kelly">Kelly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/195890-kelly?shelf=general-fiction">general-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/195890-kelly?shelf=20th-century-classics">20th-century-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3910068" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16884608868610183657">Wow! Dreiser is no joke. Make no mistake; <i> An American Tragedy </i> is long and often times obsessive in its detail. There are approximately 500 pages of exposition leading up to the climatic moment! In truth it took everything in me to finish this book and not because I wasn't enjoying the story. It just seemed like it would never end. Yet in my moments of weakness, when I felt like I just couldn't go on, the story of Clyde Griffiths and his struggle to claw his way up the American social ladder wa</span>
  <span id="freeText16884608868610183657" style="display:none">Wow! Dreiser is no joke. Make no mistake; <i> An American Tragedy </i> is long and often times obsessive in its detail. There are approximately 500 pages of exposition leading up to the climatic moment! In truth it took everything in me to finish this book and not because I wasn't enjoying the story. It just seemed like it would never end. Yet in my moments of weakness, when I felt like I just couldn't go on, the story of Clyde Griffiths and his struggle to claw his way up the American social ladder was enough to keep me going. Clyde is the perfect combination of ruthless ambition and childish naiveté. His dogged attempts to circumvent all the socio-economic factors standing in the way of his success and his eventual comeuppance made this novel worth all the effort.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16884608868610183657" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3542266687" />
    <a title="Jessaka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4659464-jessaka"><img alt="Jessaka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485709659p2/4659464.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3542266687?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessaka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessaka" href="/user/show/4659464-jessaka">Jessaka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4659464-jessaka?shelf=books-i-had-to-read-in-college">books-i-had-to-read-in-college</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3542266687" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16019713005948636176">I had to read this book in college, and when I joined Goodreads and tried to recall all of the books that I had ever read, this one came to mind, but I thought that its title was “The American Dream.” I was close. Yesterday when i saw it listed on Goodreads, it jared my memory.<br/><br/>I don’t even know what class I was in when I read this book. Nor do I remember what the professor had said. I can image, though. It was about the dream of a good life, the American dreamm a good job, a home, a car, a wife </span>
  <span id="freeText16019713005948636176" style="display:none">I had to read this book in college, and when I joined Goodreads and tried to recall all of the books that I had ever read, this one came to mind, but I thought that its title was “The American Dream.” I was close. Yesterday when i saw it listed on Goodreads, it jared my memory.<br /><br />I don’t even know what class I was in when I read this book. Nor do I remember what the professor had said. I can image, though. It was about the dream of a good life, the American dreamm a good job, a home, a car, a wife and children. <br /><br />It is strange how I recalled the names of all the books that I was made to read in college, even though I hardly liked them. There just may be something about these books that make a person remember them. <br /><br />I know that some American live the American dream. I have seen their homes, been inside. But I was never impressed. Does the American dream also include having a good marriage and great children? I think I recall something about this in the book. <br /><br />I remember my friend Paulette, a girl I knew in high school. We were walking just North of our town of Paso Robles. I wanted to see the pig farm where I had lived for a short while when I was 8 years old. On the way, she saw the old farmhouse where she had lived with her parents years ago. She said, “We were a happy then, when we were poor.” I never asked her what had happened to make it change. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16019713005948636176" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3104946274?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eileen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eileen" href="/user/show/1271901-eileen">Eileen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780451531551" href="/book/show/7371912-an-american-tragedy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1271901-eileen?shelf=2020-ng-group-challenge">2020-ng-group-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1271901-eileen?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1271901-eileen?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1271901-eileen?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1271901-eileen?shelf=2020genrechallenge">2020genrechallenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1271901-eileen?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3104946274" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15878691694325708336">4.5 stars<br/><br/>This is my first book by Theodore Dreiser and I'm glad I read it. As long as it is, I think the combination of narrator and Dreiser's style worked very well. I found myself yelling more and more at Clyde as the book kept on ramping up, but then during the trial, I started feeling sorry for him and that part of Clyde's inability to see how wrong he was and how at fault he was was because of all the lessons he was learning from society, especially high society. And yet, why did he not lea</span>
  <span id="freeText15878691694325708336" style="display:none">4.5 stars<br /><br />This is my first book by Theodore Dreiser and I'm glad I read it. As long as it is, I think the combination of narrator and Dreiser's style worked very well. I found myself yelling more and more at Clyde as the book kept on ramping up, but then during the trial, I started feeling sorry for him and that part of Clyde's inability to see how wrong he was and how at fault he was was because of all the lessons he was learning from society, especially high society. And yet, why did he not learn better lessons from his parents? Part of that is because he felt stifled by their life and he couldn't accept it as a good way to be. He was constantly trying to move up in the societal castes and problems would arrive not because of anything he did but because others were doing it to HIM. There are so many layers to this book and I can see how it would be perfect for a High School English class, except for the sheer length of the book. Although the book starts out slowly, about halfway through the book it starts to move more quickly until, by the end, you're on a runaway train. Looking back, I don't know that I would have wanted to sit down and read and study such a book, but it is definitely worthwhile! The title is completely appropriate and although times are different, I can see how this could still happen (ack!) today. The more things have changed the more they stay the same, huh? <br /><br />The extra half star is because I think the narrator does such a great job conveying the feeling of the author's words. Especially when Dreiser repeats words and phrases to convey the intensity of the moment--Dan John Miller completely captures this feeling. And the trial had me hanging by the edge of my seat, even though you just knew how it was going to all end; how it HAD to end. This was a story where, by the end, everyone is screaming and there is no way to stop it. Whew! Now I need a breather!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15878691694325708336" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <a title="Emily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily" href="/user/show/5424467-emily">Emily</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6005450549020184477">I had no idea at the time I picked this up that it is supposed to be one of the most important American novels of the 20th century—and so I read it without any preconceptions, and enjoyed it all the better for that. Despite the somewhat archaic quality of the writing I enjoyed the book, which comments on the permutations of class distinctions, equality, status and social ambition. I also enjoyed it because even though I already had a vague idea how the story was going to end (it's based on the r</span>
  <span id="freeText6005450549020184477" style="display:none">I had no idea at the time I picked this up that it is supposed to be one of the most important American novels of the 20th century—and so I read it without any preconceptions, and enjoyed it all the better for that. Despite the somewhat archaic quality of the writing I enjoyed the book, which comments on the permutations of class distinctions, equality, status and social ambition. I also enjoyed it because even though I already had a vague idea how the story was going to end (it's based on the real-life murderer Chester Gillette), I still found myself at various moments rooting for Clyde, hoping he would make the most of the opportunity his uncle had given him and prove all of society wrong. But you already know what happens from the title: this is the flip side of the American dream.</span>

<div id="review_2808995484" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2808995484" />
    <a title="Julie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14773738-julie"><img alt="Julie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509454384p2/14773738.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2808995484?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie" href="/user/show/14773738-julie">Julie</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781101100295" href="/book/show/9211335-an-american-tragedy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14773738-julie?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14773738-julie?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2808995484" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4710544573793647563">7.0/10<br/><br/>UNCLE! <br/><br/>While I am claiming a DNF on this round, I am still claiming my gold stars rating because I have already read it once. (As if once wasn't enough, she sighed.) <br/><br/>This book was really swell, boys and girls. Really it was. I mean, it was swingin'. It had all the home-grown-gee-whiz about it that everyone wants from the great american novel. Lots of dancin' and jivin' and rootin-toot-toot-tin' fun. Man, you really get to know your onions 'bout life in the bowery, guys and gals.<br/><br/>Well, that'</span>
  <span id="freeText4710544573793647563" style="display:none">7.0/10<br /><br />UNCLE! <br /><br />While I am claiming a DNF on this round, I am still claiming my gold stars rating because I have already read it once. (As if once wasn't enough, she sighed.) <br /><br />This book was really swell, boys and girls. Really it was. I mean, it was swingin'. It had all the home-grown-gee-whiz about it that everyone wants from the great american novel. Lots of dancin' and jivin' and rootin-toot-toot-tin' fun. Man, you really get to know your onions 'bout life in the bowery, guys and gals.<br /><br />Well, that's my limit of 1920s slang. I mean, I could go on -- and on -- much as Dreiser does, but I ain't in the mood, ya know. And besides, I still want to have friends when I finally leave this behind.<br /><br />I realized half way through this rich plum pudding of Americana, dense as it is, and dripping with Dreiser's peculiar brand of rum sauce, that Dreiser had a perfect (contemporaneous) counterpart in Cecil B. DeMille. In fact, I'd go out on a limb, dancin' and jivin' all the way, to say he was the literary DeMille. There is that same obsessive quality to overperform and overcomplicate; to over-describe and over-compensate. Dreiser doesn't see a room but he has to describe all the furniture in it, right down to the dust motes in the flea's eye. <br /><br />Like DeMille, he has great stories to tell and is an absolute genius for detail, which is what make the stories rich and memorable. But where DeMille had a grand canvas and knew just when to pull back the camera, Dreiser continues drilling into the center of the mote. <br /><br />He is able to capture the pathos of an entire generation with a simple, throw-away action like eyelashes fluttering for the wrong boy, at the wrong time; for within that innocuous flutter shared between the "wrong people" an entire life can come to ruin. He is a genius at depicting the unravelling, between the flutter and the fall -- and one would be hard pressed to find anyone who could do it better for it would be difficult indeed to find someone with such an exacting mind and formidable imagination. <br /><br />He is unflagging in his pursuit for truth at all costs -- for he has moved, in this novel, far beyond the yawning gape holes of Sister Carrie -- and into a world where truth is the prime, and only, mover. Despite all costs, despite all pending tragedy, each character moves in a trajectory as if on a conveyor belt to ultimate damnation. <br /><br />The true cost here, however, is wielded with a double-edged sword because while Dreiser sticks to the truth of a story, a character, he realizes there are as many truths as there are situations in a person's life; and the ultimate complication is that he tries to address each truth from different points of view. It -- truly -- redefines the meaning of omniscient narrator. <br /><br />If only Dreiser had learned to pull back the camera a bit, like deMille, for the sake of the story, the tale would hang so much better. I can forgive Dreiser much, including his abominable style, his exhaustive descriptions, his tortured prose, but the final hiccup comes when I am bidden to understand everything for everyone without being given sufficient reason. <br /><br />Still, I am very glad I read this -- at least once; and made a valiant attempt the second time 'round.<br /><br />When one wades through the prose style of possibly the worst writer in history, there is much to like, and ponder, and empathize with what happens between the flutter and the fall.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4710544573793647563" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FS6mDxGkYCJKNPoRqQkHSxByzsvPv6+VwLu0CkMbrwkZffxpSq5VtLdw5Tz+61c2EIa8xHQjsi9nLj7Al0x3SQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('wi7k4G9a6QeRZle523axnAN/GXcyfZlDCcLVifDc+NfOfb6GNFDckWwiSJSMlOHhA4treInhhPmuV19DJIsglw==')})
  }

Samuel Butler\'s autobiographical account of a harsh upbringing and troubled adulthood shines an iconoclastic light on the hypocrisy of a Victorian clerical family\'s domestic life. It also foreshadows the crumbling of nineteenth-century bourgeois ideals in the aftermath of the First World War, as well as the ways in which succeeding generations have questioned conventional values.Hailed by George Bernard Shaw as &quot;one of the summits of human achievement,&quot; this chronicle of the life and loves of Ernest Pontifex spans four generations, focusing chiefly on the relationship between Ernest and his father, Theobald. Written in the wake of Darwin\'s Origin of Species, it reflects the dawning consciousness of heredity and environment as determinants of character. Along the way, it offers a powerfully satirical indictment of Victorian England\'s major institutions—the family, the church, and the rigidly hierarchical class structure.<\/span>\n  <a       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_373755'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/373755.Absalom_Absalom_?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Absalom, Absalom!<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/3535.William_Faulkner?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">William Faulkner<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.97 avg rating &mdash; 38,939 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1936\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer11182933925164911988\">Published in 1936, Absalom, Absalom! is considered by many to be William Faulkner\'s masterpiece. Although the novel\'s complex and fragmented structure poses considerable difficulty to readers, the book\'s literary merits place it squarely in the ranks<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText11182933925164911988\" style=\"display:none\">Published in 1936, Absalom, Absalom! is considered by many to be William Faulkner\'s masterpiece. Although the novel\'s complex and fragmented structure poses considerable difficulty to readers, the book\'s literary merits place it squarely in the ranks of America\'s finest novels. The story concerns Thomas Sutpen, a poor man who finds wealth and then marries into a respectable family. His ambition and extreme need for control bring about his ruin and the ruin of his family. Sutpen\'s story is told by several narrators, allowing the reader to observe variations in the saga as it is recounted by different speakers. This unusual technique spotlights one of the novel\'s central questions: To what extent can people know the truth about the past?<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"11182933925164911988\" href=\"#\"       newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('bookCover_775366').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('bookCover_775366').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('bookCover_775366').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>

</li>
</ul>
</div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3185310388715192648">Theodore Herman Albert Dreiser was an American novelist and journalist. He pioneered the naturalist school and is known for portraying characters whose value lies not in their moral code, but in their persistence against all obstacles, and literary situations that more closely resemble studies of nature than tales of choice and agency.<br/><br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theodore_Dreiser'>http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theodore...</a></span>
